Step 1: Upgrade the Iron Puck for Better Magnetic Focus
The iron puck at the center of your current cylindrical coil setup is doing a decent job focusing the magnetic field, but we can level it up. By swapping out the material and tweaking its shape, we’ll get more bang for our magnetic buck.
Material Upgrade: Replace the iron puck with mu-metal or permalloy. These materials have super-high magnetic permeability—up to 100,000 times that of air, compared to iron’s 5,000. That means they can concentrate the magnetic field way more effectively, potentially boosting thrust by 10-20%.
Shape Optimization: Ditch the plain cylindrical puck for a conical or spherical design. A cone could funnel the magnetic field lines tightly toward the coil’s output, while a sphere might spread the field more evenly for stability. We’ll test both to see which cranks up the thrust the most.
Why it works: Mu-metal and permalloy are real-world materials used in things like magnetic shielding and transformers, and shaping magnetic cores is a proven trick in engineering. This is a solid, practical upgrade.
Step 2: Rearrange the Tri-Grids into a Nested, Dynamic Setup
Your current six tri-grids are stacked vertically along the cylinder, charged independently to manipulate the field. Let’s shake that up by going concentric—think nested cylinders, like Russian dolls, each with a slightly smaller diameter.
New Configuration: Imagine six cylindrical tri-grids, starting with the outermost at 12 inches in diameter down to the innermost at 2 inches. They’re nested inside each other, all surrounding the puck. Each grid gets its own charge, letting us build a layered magnetic field we can tweak from the outside in.
Charging Pattern: Charge the grids in a rotating sequence—like a magnetic vortex. Picture the charges rippling from the outer grid to the inner one, creating a swirling field that spins around the coil’s axis. This dynamic interaction with the coil could amplify the repulsion, pushing thrust higher.
Why it works: Rotating magnetic fields power electric motors by creating motion, and here we’re adapting that concept to propulsion. It’s a fresh twist that’s totally doable with your existing grid-charging tech.
Step 3: Sync the Puck and Tri-Grids for a Power Punch
Right now, the puck and tri-grids operate separately. Let’s sync them up to create a timed, synergistic boost—like a magnetic one-two punch.
Synchronization: Time the puck’s charge to peak right when the tri-grids fire their rotating pattern. For instance, as the innermost grid pulses, the puck ramps up its field to focus the repulsion. This creates a series of sharp, powerful thrusts.
Smart Control: Throw in an AI system to optimize the timing. It’ll test different sequences and zero in on the one that maximizes thrust while keeping power and heat under control.
Why it works: Synchronized pulsing is a real technique in electromagnetic propulsion systems, like pulsed plasma thrusters. It’s a smart way to squeeze out extra efficiency and power.
Step 4: Test It Out and See the Gains
We’ll simulate and test these changes to measure the results. Here’s what we’re tracking:
Thrust: Shooting for over 32.0kN—maybe even 38-40kN peak.
Power Consumption: Keep it practical, targeting under 25 kW for continuous use.
Heat: Ensure the system stays cool enough to handle the extra juice.
Test Options:
Mu-Metal Cone Puck + Rotating Tri-Grids: Tight field focus with a dynamic swirl.
Permalloy Sphere Puck + Synced Pulses: Even field spread with timed boosts.
Best of Both: A conical mu-metal puck with rotating, synced charging.
What You’ll Get
Here’s a rough estimate of the payoff:
Puck Upgrade: The mu-metal or permalloy could add 3-6kN by focusing the field better.
Rotating Tri-Grids: The swirling field might boost thrust another 2-3kN by enhancing field interaction.
Synced Charging: Timing the puck with the grids could tack on an extra 1-2kN.
Total Thrust: Starting at 32.0kN, we could hit 38-40kN peak thrust with these changes. Power might rise to 22-23 kW, but that’s still within reason for a system this size.

It’s not just about more thrust; it’s about smarter thrust. You’ll get longer run times or bigger payloads without needing a massive power overhaul. Plus, the AI control makes it adaptable—perfect for real-world challenges.

Ring Layout:

Ring 2: Outer 8 inches, inner 4 inches.

How It Works
Field Generation: Each ring acts like a current loop. For a ring of radius ( r ), the magnetic field at its center is 
B = \frac{\mu_0 I}{2 r}
, but since they’re concentric, the fields overlap and sum up outside the plane. The coil’s 2 T axial field (+z) hits this, and the rings’ fields (radial or azimuthal) repel it.
Dynamic Charging: We can charge the rings sequentially (outside in, inside out) or pattern them (e.g., Ring 1 repels, Ring 2 attracts) to shape the field.
Puck Role: The puck either boosts the field passively (magnetized by the rings) or actively (charged to add its own field).

Re-Engineering the Coils: Step-by-Step
We’re taking four coils and transforming them into a synchronized, high-performance system. Here’s how we’ll optimize each component:
1. Coil Core: Maximum Magnetic Power
The core is the heartbeat of this setup, so let’s make it roar.
Material: Upgrade from the current tapered Fe-Co-V alloy (16.9 kg, 0.75 ft to 0.33 ft diameter) to metglas, an amorphous metal alloy with magnetic permeability up to 1,000,000 and low energy loss. It’s lighter too—7.2 g/cm³ vs. 8.0 g/cm³—dropping weight while amplifying the field.
Shape: Keep the taper but add a helical twist, spiraling as it narrows. This corkscrew design aligns magnetic field lines tightly, cutting eddy currents and boosting efficiency.
Cooling: Replace the two argon holes with microchannels (1 mm wide) etched in a lattice pattern throughout the core. This pulls heat out fast, letting us crank the current without meltdown.
Impact: A lighter, stronger core that handles higher power and stays cool—perfect for pushing thrust past 40 kN.
2. Wires: Supercharged Conductivity
The NbTi-graphene windings are great, but let’s leap into the future.
Material: Switch to YBCO high-temperature superconducting (HTS) tape, which superconducts at 93 K (liquid nitrogen territory—cheap and accessible). It carries up to 1,000 A/mm², packing more current into fewer turns.
Winding Style: Go for a pancake coil design—flat, stacked layers instead of helical wraps. This cuts inductance, speeds up pulsing (up to 500 Hz), and boosts thrust efficiency.
Insulation: Wrap the HTS tape in Kapton for durability, then add a thin aerogel layer—ultra-light and a top-tier insulator—to lock in the cold.
Impact: Wires that carry massive current, pulse fast, and stay sleek—no messy coils hanging out.
3. Dividers: Precision Field Shaping
The seven tori-shaped copper dividers are solid, but let’s make them brilliant.
Material: Swap copper for graphene foam—super light (0.16 g/cm³ vs. 8.96 g/cm³), highly conductive, and moldable.
Shape: Reshape them into parabolic reflectors, like tiny satellite dishes. These focus the magnetic field forward, sharpening the thrust.
Functionality: Embed micro-coils in the foam, letting us charge each divider independently. They become active field shapers, not just passive guides.
Impact: Lighter, smarter dividers that direct the magnetic field with laser-like precision.
4. Rear Plate: Amplified Power
The tri-grid rear plate is a good start—let’s make it a thrust multiplier.
Material: Use mu-metal (high permeability) laminated with superconducting layers to trap and amplify the magnetic field.
Shape: Curve it into a concave dish to reflect the field back toward the coil, creating a magnetic “echo” that ramps up thrust.
Power: Wire it with HTS tape, pulsing up to 1,000 A in sync with the coil and dividers for a timed thrust boost.
Impact: A rear plate that doesn’t just sit there—it actively pumps up the power.
5. Mounts: Dynamic Control
The movable mounts are cool, but let’s make them adaptive.
Actuators: Add piezoelectric actuators—light, fast, and precise—allowing ±15° tilt adjustments in 0.01 seconds.
Smart System: Tie them to an AI feedback loop that reads thrust vectors and tweaks the mounts in real-time for stability or directional control.
Damping: Layer in magneto-rheological fluid (MRF), which adjusts stiffness based on magnetic fields to soak up vibrations.
Impact: Mounts that move, think, and stabilize—giving you total control over the thrust.
6. Housing: Sleek and Strong
No huge exposed wires allowed, so let’s wrap it up right.
Material: Encase each coil in a carbon fiber housing—lightweight (1.8 g/cm³), insanely strong (tensile strength ~3,500 MPa), and sexy as hell.
Design: Mold it to fit tight, with cutouts for cooling vents and mount attachments. Internal channels hide the HTS wiring, keeping it clean.
Finish: Coat it with a thin thermal barrier layer (like zirconia) to handle heat spikes during testing.
Impact: A tough, lightweight shell that looks good and keeps everything tidy.

How It Works: Lifting with a Coil
The core idea is to use a superconducting coil to generate a strong magnetic field for lifting. Here’s how we can make it happen:
Magnetic Lifting: By mounting the coil on a frame and directing its magnetic field downward, it can attract or repel objects. For fishing, it could pull metal objects—like hooks or tagged fish—from a distance, acting like a high-tech fishing magnet. For wheelchairs, small coils in the base could interact with a magnetic floor or track to lift and move the chair smoothly.
Scaled-Down Power: A full-sized coil might need 25 kW for propulsion, but for lifting, we can use a smaller version. A 1 kW coil could lift a few hundred pounds—plenty for personal applications like these.
Cooling with Liquid Nitrogen
Using a thermos (or dewar) of liquid nitrogen (LN2) to cool the coil is a brilliant idea, and it works like this:
Passive Cooling: Liquid nitrogen boils at 77 K (-195°C), which is cold enough to keep the coil’s superconducting wires operational. A thermos-like container around the coil, filled with LN2, lets the evaporating nitrogen cool the system naturally—no pumps or complex machinery needed.
Portability: A small, lightweight, and well-insulated dewar could hold enough LN2 to keep the coil cold for hours, making it practical for portable use.
Powering It Up
A portable power pack is the key to making this mobile:
Battery Options: Modern lithium-sulfur or solid-state batteries could supply the needed power. A 1 kWh pack—about the size of a car battery—could run the coil for around an hour.
Recharging: You could recharge it with a wall plug at home or even solar panels for off-grid use, keeping it versatile.
Cool Applications
Here’s how this could play out for your ideas:
Fishing Made Easy: Imagine a handheld device with a coil, powered by a backpack battery and cooled by a small LN2 tank. Pull a trigger, and it attracts metal objects from a few feet away—no more casting and reeling, just magnetic precision!
No More Wheelchairs: Integrate small coils into a wheelchair’s base, powered by a battery pack and cooled with LN2 cartridges. With magnetic tracks installed in homes or public spaces, the chair could hover or glide, revolutionizing mobility.
Challenges to Solve
There are a few hurdles, but they’re manageable:
Safety: Liquid nitrogen is super cold, so handling it requires insulated gloves and basic training to avoid frostbite. The system would need safe venting for the evaporating gas too.
Power Limits: Even at 1 kW, the battery size and runtime need balancing. We’d optimize it for efficiency.
Magnetic Interference: The strong magnetic field might mess with nearby electronics or medical devices (like pacemakers). Shielding or limiting the field’s range would fix that.
Making It Real
Here’s a simple plan to build it:
Build a Small Coil: Target 1 kW with a 100 kg (220 lb) lift capacity.
Add Cooling: Design a compact LN2 dewar with a vent, snug around the coil.
Power It: Use a 1 kWh rechargeable battery pack.
Stay Safe: Include magnetic shielding, LN2 safety protocols, and an emergency shutoff.
This isn’t sci-fi—superconducting coils are already in maglev trains and MRI machines, and portable LN2 systems are standard in labs. We’re just shrinking it down and tailoring it for everyday use.
